The insertion time (<i>T</i>) of an LTR retrotransposon was calculated using the formula <i>T</i>=<i>K</i>/2<i>r</i>, where <i>K</i> is the distance and <i>r</i> is the rate of nucleotide substitution, which was set to 7.0 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions per site per year according to Ossowski <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2130">47</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Protein-coding gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>The repeat-masked spinach genome was used for gene prediction using MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2142">48</a></sup> (v2.31.6), which combines evidence from <i>ab initio</i>, transcript mapping and protein homology-based predictions to define the confident gene models. SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2149">49</a></sup> (v2009-02-03) and AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Stanke, M., Tzvetkova, A. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS at EGASP: using EST, protein and genomic alignments for improved gene prediction in the human genome. Genome Biol. 7, S11.1–8 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2153">50</a></sup> (v3.0.2) were used for <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions. For transcript mapping, RNA-Seq data were aligned to the spinach genome using Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2161">51</a></sup> (v2.0.13; parameters ‘--read-mismatches 1 --splice-mismatches 0 --min-intron-length 30’) and then assembled using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2165">52</a></sup> (v2.2.1; parameters ‘--no-effective-length-correction --min-intron-length 30 --min-frags-per-transfrag 5’). For homologous protein mapping, protein sequences from sugar beet, Arabidopsis, potato, tomato and Swiss-Prot were aligned to the genome using Spaln<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Iwata, H. &amp; Gotoh, O. Benchmarking spliced alignment programs including Spaln2, an extended version of Spaln that incorporates additional species-specific features. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e161 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2169">53</a></sup> (v2.1.4; parameters ‘-Xk11 -H35’). For predicted genes without a transcript or homologous protein support, they were compared against the Pfam database (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org">http://pfam.xfam.org</a>) and those that did not contain any Pfam domains were discarded. Furthermore, genes highly homologous to transposable elements were also removed. Predicted genes with expression levels of at least one RPKM (reads per kilobase of exon model per million mapped reads) in at least one of the 120 spinach accessions were considered as supported by the RNA-Seq data.</p><p>To annotate the spinach predicted genes, their protein sequences were compared to GenBank nr, the Arabidopsis protein and UniProt (Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL; <a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>) databases using BLAST (parameter ‘-evalue 1e-4’), as well as the InterPro database using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2190">54</a></sup> (v5.10–50.0). GO annotations were obtained using Blast2GO (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2193">55</a>) (version 2.5.0) based on the BLAST results against the GenBank nr database and results from the InterProScan analysis. Functional descriptions were assigned to spinach genes using AHRD (v3.3; <a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>). Enzyme-encoding genes were extracted based on the AHRD result and Enzyme Commission (EC) information from the Blast2GO analysis. Transcription factors and protein kinases were identified and classified into different families using the iTAK pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Zheng, Y. et al. iTAK: a program for genome-wide prediction and classification of plant transcription factors, transcriptional regulators, and protein kinases. Mol. Plant 9, 1667–1670 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2204">56</a></sup> (v1.6).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Comparative genomic analyses</h3><p>Orthologous groups were constructed using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2216">57</a></sup> (v1.4; inflation parameter: 1.5) with an all -versus-all BLASTP comparison (parameters ‘-evalue 1e-5 -max_target_seqs 1000’) of protein sequences from spinach and ten other plant species, including <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, rice, sugar beet, tomato, grape, <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, watermelon, cacao, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and papaya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms15275#MOESM25">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Protein sequences of single-gene families were aligned by MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2233">45</a></sup> (v3.8.31). The alignments were trimmed by the trimAL program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Capella-Gutierrez, S., Silla-Martinez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldon, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2237">58</a></sup> (v1.2rev59) with the parameter ‘gt’ set to 0.9. The concatenated protein alignments were used to construct a phylogenetic tree using the maximum parsimony method implemented in MEGA6 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725–2729 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2240">59</a>) with 1,000 bootstrap replications. Syntenies within and between spinach, sugar beet and Arabidopsis genomes were detected using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2244">60</a></sup> (parameters ‘-s 5 -e 5’). <i>Ks</i> values of homologous gene pairs in the syntenic regions were calculated and the speciation time base on <i>Ks</i> values was derived using the equation <i>T</i>=<i>Ks</i>/2<i>r</i> with <i>r</i>=7.0 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions per site per year according to Ossowski <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2273">47</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Transcriptome sequencing of spinach germplasm collection</h3><p>A total of 120 cultivated (<i>S. oleracea</i>) and wild (<i>S. tetrandra</i> and <i>S. turkestanica</i>) spinach accessions including nine that were recently reported by Xu <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Xu, C. et al. De novo and comparative transcriptome analysis of cultivated and wild spinach. Sci. Rep. 5, 17706 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2297">11</a></sup> were collected, among which 51 were provided by the North Central Regional Plant Introduction Station (NCRPIS), Ames, Iowa, and 69 by seed companies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms15275#MOESM25">Supplementary Data 1</a>). Sample collection, total RNA extraction and RNA-Seq library construction were same as described in Xu <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Xu, C. et al. De novo and comparative transcriptome analysis of cultivated and wild spinach. Sci. Rep. 5, 17706 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2308">11</a></sup>. Specifically, the entire 20-day-old seedlings were collected and stored at −80 °C till use. Total RNA was extracted using the QIAGEN RNeasy Plant Mini Kit following the manufacturer’s instructions (QIAGEN, Valencia, CA, USA). The quality and quantity of RNA were assessed by electrophoresis on 1% agarose gels and by a NanoDrop 1000 spectrophotometer (Thermo Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA), respectively. The strand-specific RNA-Seq libraries were constructed for each accession following the protocol described in Zhong <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Zhong, S. et al. High-throughput Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing library preparation. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 2011, 940–949 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2315">61</a></sup> and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform (Illumina Inc., USA) using the single-end mode with the read length of 100 bp.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">RNA-Seq data analysis and SNP calling</h3><p>Raw RNA-Seq reads were processed using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2327">29</a></sup> (v0.33; parameters ‘SLIDINGWINDOW:4:20 LEADING:3 TRAILING:3 MINLEN:40’) to remove adaptor and low quality sequences. Reads longer than 40 bp were kept and then aligned to the ribosomal RNA database (<a href="https://www.arb-silva.de/">https://www.arb-silva.de/</a>) using bowtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2338">62</a></sup> (v1.0.0; parameter ‘-v 3’) and the aligned reads were discarded. For gene expression analysis, the resulting high-quality cleaned reads were aligned to the assembled spinach genome using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2342">51</a></sup> allowing two mismatches. Following alignments, raw counts for each spinach gene were derived and normalized to RPKM.</p><p>To identify SNPs and small indels among the 120 spinach accessions, raw RNA-Seq reads were first processed to collapse duplicated reads into unique reads and remove adaptor and low quality sequences. The resulting reads were aligned to the spinach genome using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2349">63</a></sup> (v0.7.12-r1039; parameters ‘-n 0.04 -o 1 -e 2’). Only reads uniquely mapped (having a single best match) to the genome were kept. Following mapping, SNPs and small indels were identified based on the mpileup files generated by SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2353">64</a></sup> (v1.1). The identified SNPs and small indels were supported by at least three distinct reads and each genotype had an allele frequency &gt;25%.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Phylogenetic and population structure analyses</h3><p>Only SNPs with minor allele frequency (MAF) &gt;5% and missing data &lt;10% (a total of 11,434) were used for phylogenetic and population structure analyses. A subset of 2,769 SNPs at fourfold-degenerate sites was used to construct a maximum parsimony phylogenetic tree using PAUP*<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Swofford, D. L. PAUP*. Phylogenetic analysis using parsimony (*and other methods). Version 4 Sinauer Associates (2002) 
                    http://paup.scs.fsu.edu/Cmd_ref_v2.pdf
                    
                  ." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2365">65</a></sup> (parameters ‘search=heuristic start=stepwise addseq=random swap=tbr’) with 100 bootstrap replications. Two <i>S. tetrandra</i> accessions, Sp42 and Sp43, were used as the outgroup because of their larger genetic distance from the cultivars. Principal component analysis was performed using the 11,434 SNPs with the EIGENSOFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Price, A. L. et al. Principal components analysis corrects for stratification in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 38, 904–909 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2372">66</a></sup> smartpca (v13050) program with the parameter ‘-k’ set to 10. Population structure analysis was performed using the program STRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Pritchard, J. K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945–959 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2376">23</a></sup> (v2.3.4). To determine the most likely group number, STRUCTURE was run 20 times on 1,000 randomly selected SNPs for each <i>K</i> value from 1 to 20. The optimal <i>K</i> was selected using the Evanno method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Evanno, G., Regnaut, S. &amp; Goudet, J. Detecting the number of clusters of individuals using the software STRUCTURE: a simulation study. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2611–2620 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2387">67</a></sup>. After determining the Δ<i>K</i>, the subgroup membership of each accession was determined by 15,000 iterations for each <i>K</i> (<i>K</i>= 2 to 20) using the 11,434 SNPs.</p><p><i>π</i> was used to calculate nucleotide diversities in each <i>Spinacia</i> group. SNPs with genotype information in at least 90% of the accessions were used in the calculation. A sliding window approach was used to calculate <i>π</i> in the assembled spinach genome with a window size of 10 kb and a step size of 1 kb. LD decay was calculated based on the correlation coefficient (<i>r</i><sup><i>2</i></sup>) of alleles using Haploview<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Barrett, J. C., Fry, B., Maller, J. &amp; Daly, M. J. Haploview: analysis and visualization of LD and haplotype maps. Bioinformatics 21, 263–265 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2417">68</a></sup> (v4.2; parameters ‘-dprime -maxdistance 1000 -minMAF 0.05 -hwcutoff 0.001’).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Selective sweep and population differentiation</h3><p>Whole-genome screening of selective sweeps was performed by comparing allele frequency differentiation between <i>S. turkestanica</i> and <i>S. oleracea</i> using XP-CLR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Chen, H., Patterson, N. &amp; Reich, D. Population differentiation as a test for selective sweeps. Genome Res. 20, 393–402 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2436">27</a></sup> (v1.0), a method based on modelling the likelihood of multilocus allele frequency differentiation between two populations. Genetic distances between SNPs were interpolated according to their physical distances in our genetic map. XP-CLR was run for each pseudochromosome or scaffold with parameters ‘-w1 0.0005 100 100 -p0 0.7’. Final estimates were tabulated in non-overlapping 10-kb windows across the genome. Adjacent windows with high XP-CLR scores (top 3%) were grouped into a single region, which was then given a score using the maximum of region-wise XP-CLR. Regions with top 1% of XP-CLR scores were considered as potential selected sweeps. We further calculated the <i>π</i> ratios between <i>S. turkestanica</i> and <i>S. oleracea</i> (<i>π</i><sub>w</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>c</sub>) in 10-kb sliding windows with a step size of 1 kb and identified regions with top 50% highest <i>π</i> ratios. Three selective sweeps derived from the XP-CLR analysis were not shared with those by the <i>π</i> ratio approach and thus excluded from the final results of the putative selected regions.</p><p>The population fixation index <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> was calculated using a sliding window approach (10-kb sliding windows with a step size of 1 kb) using the R package HIERFSTAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Goudet, J. hierfstat, a package for R to compute and test hierarchical F-statistics. Mol. Ecol. Notes 5, 184–186 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2471">69</a></sup> (v0.04-22). Genomic regions with top 1% of <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values between <i>S. oleracea</i> and <i>S. turkestanica</i> were identified as potential differentiated regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Genome-wide association study of bolting</h3><p>Bolting trait of 59 spinach accessions (20–30 replications) were evaluated in the greenhouse at Shanghai Normal University in the spring of 2015. Bolting time was monitored daily and determined as the number of days from planting to the first elongation of the floral stem observed in at least half of the used plants for each accession. Correction for population stratification and genome-wide association study for the bolting trait were performed using EIGENSOFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Price, A. L. et al. Principal components analysis corrects for stratification in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 38, 904–909 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms15275#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d45589e2494">66</a></sup> (version 6.0.1; parameters ‘-q YES -k 10’), and using a total of 11,434 SNPs (MAF&gt;5% and missing data &lt;10%). SNPs with <i>P</i>&lt;0.0001 were considered significantly associated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Data availability</h3><p>This Whole Genome Shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession LZYP00000000. The version described in this paper is version LZYP01000000. Raw sequence reads of genome and transcriptome sequencing have been deposited in NCBI sequence read archive (SRA) under accession number SRP076521. The spinach genome sequence and the annotation are also available at SpinachBase (<a href="http://www.spinachbase.org">http://www.spinachbase.org</a>). The authors declare that all other data supporting the findings of this study are available from the corresponding authors on request.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec24" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec24-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec24">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec24-content"><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Xu, C. <i>et al</i>. Draft genome of spinach and transcriptome diversity of 120 <i>Spinacia</i> accessions. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> <b>8,</b> 15275 doi: 10.1038/ncomms15275 (2017).</p><p><b>Publisher’s note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section>
